<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Ma Sanbao" redirects here. href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table><p><b>Zheng He</b> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">郑和</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">鄭和</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Zhènghé</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Chêng-ho</span></i>; 1371–1433 or 1435) was a Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Chinese_navy" title="History of the Chinese navy">mariner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_exploration" title="Chinese exploration">explorer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_imperial_China" title="Foreign relations of imperial China">diplomat</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fleet_admiral" title="Fleet admiral">fleet admiral</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Court_eunuch" title="Court eunuch">court eunuch</a> during China's <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Ming_dynasty" title="History of the Ming dynasty">early Ming dynasty</a>. He was originally born as <b>Ma He</b> in a <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_China" title="Islam in China">Muslim</a> family and later adopted the surname Zheng conferred by the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200722–23_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200722–23-2">[2]</a></sup> Commissioned by the Yongle Emperor and later the <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande Emperor</a>, Zheng commanded seven expeditionary <a href="/wiki/Ming_treasure_voyages" title="Ming treasure voyages">treasure voyages</a> to <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">West Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> from 1405 to 1433. According to legend, his larger ships carried hundreds of sailors on four decks and were almost twice as long as any wooden ship ever recorded.
</p><p>As a favorite of the Yongle Emperor, whom Zheng assisted in the <a href="/wiki/Jingnan_campaign" title="Jingnan campaign">overthrow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Jianwen_Emperor" title="Jianwen Emperor">Jianwen Emperor</a>, he rose to the top of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Ming_Empire" title="Government of the Ming Empire">imperial hierarchy</a> and served as commander of the southern capital <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family">Early life and family</span></h2>
<p>Zheng He was born <b>Ma He</b> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">馬和</span></span>) to a <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_China" title="Islam in China">Muslim</a> family of <a href="/wiki/Kunyang_Subdistrict" title="Kunyang Subdistrict">Kunyang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunming" title="Kunming">Kunming</a>, Yunnan, during the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200711,_148Mills19705Ray198766Levathes199661_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200711,_148Mills19705Ray198766Levathes199661-3">[3]</a></sup>  He had an older brother and four sisters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200711Mills19705Levathes199662Perkins2000621_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200711Mills19705Levathes199662Perkins2000621-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zheng He's religious beliefs were <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">eclectic</a> in his adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERay198766Dreyer2007148_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERay198766Dreyer2007148-5">[5]</a></sup> The Liujiagang and Changle inscriptions suggest that devotion to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Tianfei</a>, the patron goddess of sailors and seafarers, was the dominant faith to which he adhered, reflecting the goddess's central role to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_fleet" title="Chinese treasure fleet">treasure fleet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> John Guy mentions, "When Zheng He, the Muslim eunuch leader of the great expeditions to the 'Western Ocean' (Indian Ocean) in the early fifteenth century, embarked on his voyages, it was from the Divine Woman that he sought protection, as well as at the tombs of the Muslim saints on Lingshan Hill, above the city of Quanzhou."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zheng He was a great-great-great-grandson of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sayyid_Ajjal_Shams_al-Din_Omar" title="Sayyid Ajjal Shams al-Din Omar">Sayyid Ajjal Shams al-Din Omar</a>, who served in the administration of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> and was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governor_of_Yunnan" title="Governor of Yunnan">governor of Yunnan</a> during the early <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> His great-grandfather Bayan may have been stationed at a Mongol garrison in Yunnan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills19705_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills19705-10">[10]</a></sup> Zheng He's grandfather carried the title <i><a href="/wiki/Hajji" title="Hajji">hajji</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200711Levathes199661–62_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200711Levathes199661–62-11">[11]</a></sup> and his father had the <a href="/wiki/Sinicization" title="Sinicization">sinicized</a> surname Ma and the title <i>hajji</i>, which suggests that they had made the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">pilgrimage to Mecca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200711Mills19705Levathes199661–62_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200711Mills19705Levathes199661–62-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the autumn of 1381, a <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming</a> army invaded and <a href="/wiki/Ming_conquest_of_Yunnan" title="Ming conquest of Yunnan">conquered Yunnan</a>, which was then ruled by the Mongol prince <a href="/wiki/Basalawarmi" title="Basalawarmi">Basalawarmi</a>, Prince of Liang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1381, Ma Hajji, Zheng He's father, died in the fighting between the Ming armies and Mongol forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199662_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199662-14">[14]</a></sup> Dreyer states that Zheng He's father died at 39 while he resisted the Ming conquest, while Levathes states that Zheng He's father died at 37, but it is unclear if he was helping the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_Army" title="Mongol Army">Mongol Army</a> or was just caught in the onslaught of battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199662_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199662-14">[14]</a></sup> Wenming, the oldest son, buried their father outside Kunming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199662_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199662-14">[14]</a></sup> In his capacity as Admiral, Zheng He had an epitaph engraved in honour of his father, composed by the Minister of Rites Li Zhigang on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duanwu_Festival" title="Duanwu Festival">Duanwu Festival</a> of the third year in the Yongle era (1 June 1405).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199662–63_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199662–63-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Capture.2C_castration_and_service"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Capture,_castration_and_service">Capture, castration and service</span></h2>
<p>Zheng He was captured by the Ming armies at Yunnan in 1381.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199662_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199662-14">[14]</a></sup> General <a href="/wiki/Fu_Youde" title="Fu Youde">Fu Youde</a> saw Ma He on a road and approached him to inquire about the location of the Mongol pretender. Ma He responded defiantly by saying that the Mongol pretender had jumped into a lake. Afterwards, the general took him prisoner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199658_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199658-16">[16]</a></sup> He was <a href="/wiki/Castration" title="Castration">castrated</a> at some point between the age of 10 and 14,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199658_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199658-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712_&amp;_16_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712_&amp;_16-17">[17]</a></sup> and was placed in the service of the Prince of Yan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712_&amp;_16_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712_&amp;_16-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ma He was sent to serve in the household of Zhu Di, the Prince of Yan, who later became the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712Levathes199658_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712Levathes199658-18">[18]</a></sup> Zhu Di was eleven years older than Ma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200718_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200718-19">[19]</a></sup> Enslaved as a eunuch servant, Ma He eventually gained the confidence of Zhu Di, who, as his benefactor, would gain the allegiance and loyalty of the young eunuch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Since 1380, the prince had been governing Beiping (later <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712-13">[13]</a></sup> which was near the northern frontier, with hostile Mongol tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199658Dreyer200718_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199658Dreyer200718-21">[21]</a></sup> Ma would spend his early life as a soldier on the northern frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199658Dreyer200716_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199658Dreyer200716-22">[22]</a></sup> He often participated in Zhu Di's military campaigns against the Mongols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200718Levathes199664_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200718Levathes199664-23">[23]</a></sup> On 2 March 1390, Ma accompanied the Prince when he commanded <a href="/wiki/Ming_campaign_against_the_Uriankhai" title="Ming campaign against the Uriankhai">his first expedition</a>, which was a great victory, as the Mongol commander <a href="/wiki/Naghachu" title="Naghachu">Naghachu</a> surrendered as soon as he realized he had fallen for a deception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199664–66_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199664–66-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG/220px-Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG/330px-Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG/440px-Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ma Hajji, a Yuan Dynasty official in Yunnan (a descendant of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sayyid_Ajjal_Shams_al-Din_Omar" title="Sayyid Ajjal Shams al-Din Omar">Sayyid Ajjal Shams al-Din Omar</a>), and his young son Ma He, the future Admiral Zheng He, as imagined by a modern <a href="/wiki/Kunyang_Subdistrict" title="Kunyang Subdistrict">Kunyang</a> sculptor.</div></div></div>
<p>Eventually, he gained the confidence and trust of the prince.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200718_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200718-19">[19]</a></sup> Ma was also known as "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/s%C4%81nb%C7%8Eo" title="wikt:sānbǎo">Sanbao</a>" during his service in the household of the Prince of Yan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199663_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199663-25">[25]</a></sup> This name was a reference to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_Jewels" title="Three Jewels">Three Jewels</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%89%E5%AF%B6" title="wikt:三寶">三寶</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Sānbǎo</span></i>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Triratna" title="Triratna">triratna</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200712-13">[13]</a></sup> This name could also be written <span lang="zh-Hant">三保</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Sānbǎo</span></i>, literally "Three Protections."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[note 1]</a></sup> Ma received a proper education at Beiping, which he would not have had if he had been placed in the imperial capital, Nanjing, as the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu" title="Hongwu">Hongwu</a> Emperor did not trust eunuchs and believed that it was better to keep them illiterate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199663_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199663-25">[25]</a></sup> The Hongwu Emperor purged and exterminated many of the original Ming leadership and gave his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enfeoffment" title="Enfeoffment">enfeoffed</a> sons more military authority, especially those in the north, like the Prince of Yan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200719_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200719-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adulthood_and_military_career">Adulthood and military career</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:350px; border-width: 1px; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>The power of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianfei" title="Tianfei">goddess</a>, having indeed been manifested in previous times, has been abundantly revealed in the present generation. In the midst of the rushing waters it happened that, when there was a hurricane, suddenly a <a href="/wiki/St._Elmo%27s_fire" title="St. Elmo's fire">divine lantern</a> was seen shining at the masthead, and as soon as that miraculous light appeared the danger was appeased, so that even in the peril of capsizing one felt reassured and that there was no cause for fear.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">— <i>Admiral Zheng He and his associates (Changle inscription) about witnessing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianfei" title="Tianfei">Tianfei</a>'s divine lantern, which represented the natural phenomena <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Elmo%27s_fire" title="Saint Elmo's fire">Saint Elmo's fire</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Zheng He's appearance as an adult was recorded: he was seven <i><a href="/wiki/Chi_(unit)" title="Chi (unit)">chi</a></i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[note 2]</a></sup> tall, had a waist that was five <i>chi</i> in circumference, cheeks and a forehead that was high, a small nose, glaring eyes, teeth that were white and well-shaped as shells, and a voice that was as loud as a bell. It is also recorded that he had great knowledge about warfare and was well-accustomed to battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199662Dreyer200718–19_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199662Dreyer200718–19-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The young eunuch eventually became a trusted adviser to the prince and assisted him when the <a href="/wiki/Jianwen_Emperor" title="Jianwen Emperor">Jianwen Emperor</a>'s hostility to his uncle's feudal bases prompted the 1399–1402 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jingnan_Campaign" title="Jingnan Campaign">Jingnan Campaign</a>, which ended with the emperor's apparent death and the ascension of Zhu Di, Prince of Yan, as the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a>. In 1393, the Crown Prince had died, thus the deceased prince's son became the new heir apparent. By the time the emperor died (24 June 1398), the Prince of Qin and the Prince of Jin had perished, which left Zhu Di, the Prince of Yan, as the eldest surviving son of the emperor. However, Zhu Di's nephew succeeded the imperial throne as the <a href="/wiki/Jianwen_Emperor" title="Jianwen Emperor">Jianwen Emperor</a>. In 1398, he issued a policy known as <i>xuēfān</i> (<span lang="zh">削藩</span>), or "reducing the feudatories", which entails eliminating all princes by stripping their power and military forces. In August 1399, Zhu Di openly rebelled against his nephew. In 1399, Ma He successfully defended Beiping's city reservoir Zhenglunba against the imperial armies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200719–23Levathes199667,_72–73_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200719–23Levathes199667,_72–73-31">[29]</a></sup> In January 1402, Zhu Di began with his military campaign to capture the imperial capital Nanjing. Zheng He would be one of his commanders during that campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199670_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199670-32">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1402, Zhu Di's armies defeated the imperial forces and marched into Nanjing on 13 July 1402.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199670Dreyer200721–22_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199670Dreyer200721–22-33">[31]</a></sup> Zhu Di accepted the elevation to emperor four days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200721–22_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200721–22-34">[32]</a></sup> After ascending the throne as the Yongle Emperor, Zhu Di promoted Ma He as the Grand Director (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">太監</span></span>, <i>tàijiān</i>) of the Directorate of Palace Servants (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">内宫監</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200721–22_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200721–22-34">[32]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">Chinese New Year</a> on 11 February 1404, the Yongle Emperor conferred the surname "Zheng" to Ma He, because he had distinguished himself defending the city reservoir Zhenglunba against imperial forces in the Siege of Beiping of 1399.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200722–23Levathes199672–73_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200722–23Levathes199672–73-35">[33]</a></sup> Another reason was that the eunuch commander also distinguished himself during the 1402 campaign to capture the capital, Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199672–73_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199672–73-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the new administration, Zheng He served in the highest posts as Grand Director and later as Chief Envoy (<span lang="zh-Hant">正使</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zhèngshǐ</span></i>) during his sea voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills19705Levathes199661–63Perkins2000621_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills19705Levathes199661–63Perkins2000621-37">[35]</a></sup> Over the next three decades he conducted seven of the voyages on behalf of the emperor of trading and collecting tribute in the eastern Pacific and Indian Oceans.
</p><p>In 1424, Zheng He traveled to <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">Palembang</a> in Sumatra to confer an official seal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[note 3]</a></sup> and letter of appointment upon Shi Jisun, who was placed in the office of Pacification Commissioner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200795_&amp;_136_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200795_&amp;_136-40">[37]</a></sup> The <i>Taizong Shilu</i> 27 February 1424 entry reports that Shi Jisun had sent Qiu Yancheng as envoy to petition the approval of the succession from his father Shi Jinqing, who was the Pacification Commissioner of Palembang, and was given permission from the Yongle Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200795_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200795-41">[38]</a></sup> On 7 September 1424, Zhu Gaozhi had inherited the throne as the Hongxi Emperor after the death of the Yongle Emperor on 12 August 1424.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007136–137Duyvendak1938388_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007136–137Duyvendak1938388-42">[39]</a></sup> When Zheng He returned from Palembang, he found that the Yongle Emperor had died during his absence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200795,_136–137Duyvendak1938387_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200795,_136–137Duyvendak1938387-43">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 7 September 1424, the <a href="/wiki/Hongxi_Emperor" title="Hongxi Emperor">Hongxi Emperor</a> terminated the undertaking of further treasure voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007137_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007137-44">[41]</a></sup> On 24 February 1425, he appointed Zheng He as the defender of Nanjing and ordered him to continue his command over the treasure fleet for the city's defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007139–140_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007139–140-45">[42]</a></sup> On 25 March 1428, the Xuande Emperor ordered Zheng He and others to take over the supervision for the rebuilding and repair of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Bao%27en_Temple" title="Great Bao'en Temple">Great Bao'en Temple</a> at Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007142_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007142-46">[43]</a></sup> He completed the construction of the temple in 1431.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007135_&amp;_144_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007135_&amp;_144-47">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 15 May 1426, the <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande Emperor</a> ordered the Directorate of Ceremonial to send a letter to Zheng He to reprimand him for a transgression. Earlier, an official<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[note 4]</a></sup> petitioned the emperor to reward workmen who had built temples in Nanjing. The Xuande Emperor responded negatively to the official for placing the costs to the court instead of the monks themselves, but he realized that Zheng He and his associates had instigated the official. According to <a href="#CITEREFDreyer2007">Dreyer (2007)</a>, the nature of the emperor's words indicated that Zheng He's behaviour in the situation was the last straw, but there is too little information about what had happened earlier. Nevertheless, the Xuande Emperor would eventually come to trust Zheng He.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007141–142_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007141–142-50">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1430, the new <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande Emperor</a> appointed Zheng He to command over a seventh and final expedition into the "Western Ocean" (Indian Ocean). In 1431, Zheng He was bestowed with the title <i>Sanbao Taijian</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%89%E5%AF%B6" title="wikt:三寶">三寶</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A4%AA%E7%9B%A3" title="wikt:太監">太監</a></span>), using his informal name Sanbao and the title of Grand Director.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills19706–7_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills19706–7-51">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Expeditions">Expeditions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ming_treasure_voyages" title="Ming treasure voyages">Ming treasure voyages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ZhengHeShips.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="426" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/ZhengHeShips.gif/220px-ZhengHeShips.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/ZhengHeShips.gif/330px-ZhengHeShips.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/ZhengHeShips.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ZhengHeShips.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 17th-century Chinese <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodblock</a> print, thought to represent Zheng He's ships</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> and the expanding Sino-Arab trade during the 14th century had gradually expanded Chinese knowledge of the world since "universal" maps previously displaying only China and its surrounding seas began to expand farther and farther southwest, with much more accurate depictions of the extent of Arabia and Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang1974_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang1974-52">[48]</a></sup> Between 1405 and 1433, the Ming government sponsored seven naval expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[49]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a>, disregarding the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang_Ming_Zuxun" title="Huang Ming Zuxun">expressed wishes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuiChunHing_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-HuiChunHing-54">[50]</a></sup> designed them to establish a Chinese presence and impose imperial control over the Indian Ocean trade, impress foreign peoples in the Indian Ocean basin, and extend the empire's tributary system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It has also been inferred from passages in the <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Ming" title="History of Ming">History of Ming</a></i> that the initial voyages were launched as part of the emperor's attempt to capture his <a href="/wiki/Jianwen_Emperor" title="Jianwen Emperor">escaped predecessor</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang1974_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang1974-52">[48]</a></sup> which would have made the first voyage the "largest-scale manhunt on water in the history of China."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeng200513_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeng200513-55">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zheng He was placed as the admiral in control of the huge fleet and armed forces that undertook the expeditions. <a href="/wiki/Wang_Jinghong" title="Wang Jinghong">Wang Jinghong</a> was appointed as second in command. Preparations were thorough and wide-ranging, including the use of so many linguists that a foreign language institute was established at Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang1974_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang1974-52">[48]</a></sup> Zheng He's first voyage departed 11 July 1405, from <a href="/wiki/Suzhou" title="Suzhou">Suzhou</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsai2002_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsai2002-56">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 203">: 203 </span></sup> and consisted of a fleet of 317<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-57">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tamura_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tamura-59">[55]</a></sup> ships holding almost 28,000 crewmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-57">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zheng He's fleets visited <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siam" title="Siam">Siam</a> (Thailand), <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, India, the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup> dispensing and receiving goods along the way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tamura_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tamura-59">[55]</a></sup> Zheng He presented gifts of gold, silver, <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>, and silk, and in return, China received such novelties as <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">ostriches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zebra" title="Zebra">zebras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camels</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili Coast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsai2002_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tsai2002-56">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 206">: 206 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tamura_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tamura-59">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-64">[60]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Giraffe" title="Giraffe">giraffe</a> that he brought back from <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> was considered to be a <i><a href="/wiki/Qilin" title="Qilin">qilin</a></i> and taken as proof of the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">Mandate of Heaven</a> upon the administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuyvendak1938402_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuyvendak1938402-65">[61]</a></sup> The Daxuexi Alley Mosque in Xi'an has a stele dating to January 1523, inscribed with Zheng He's the fourth maritime voyage to Tianfang, Arabian Peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyages_of_Zheng_He.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="792" data-file-width="1126" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Voyages_of_Zheng_He.png/220px-Voyages_of_Zheng_He.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Voyages_of_Zheng_He.png/330px-Voyages_of_Zheng_He.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Voyages_of_Zheng_He.png/440px-Voyages_of_Zheng_He.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voyages_of_Zheng_He.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Voyages of Zheng He</div></div></div>
<p>While Zheng He's fleet was unprecedented, the routes were not. His fleet followed long-established, well-mapped routes of trade between China and the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[49]</a></sup> that had been used since at least the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>. That fact, along with the use of a more-than-abundant number of crew members who were regular military personnel, leads some to speculate that the expeditions may have been geared at least partially at spreading China's power through expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> Period, the king of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wu_(Ten_Kingdoms)" title="Wu (Ten Kingdoms)">Wu</a> sent a 20-year diplomatic mission led by Zhu Ying and Kang Tai along the coast of Asia, which reached as far as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeng200512_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeng200512-67">[63]</a></sup> After centuries of disruption, the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> restored large-scale maritime trade from China in the South Pacific and Indian Oceans and reached as far as the Arabian Peninsula and East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeng2005_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeng2005-68">[64]</a></sup> When his fleet first arrived at <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a>, there was already a sizable Chinese community. The <i>General Survey of the Ocean Shores</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">瀛涯勝覽</span></span>, <i>Yíngyá Shènglǎn</i>), composed by the translator <a href="/wiki/Ma_Huan" title="Ma Huan">Ma Huan</a> in 1416, gives very detailed accounts of his observations of people's customs and lives in the ports that they visited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills1970_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills1970-69">[65]</a></sup> He referred to the expatriate Chinese as "<a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a>" people (<span lang="zh-Hant">唐人</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Tángrén</span></i>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KangnidoMap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/KangnidoMap.jpg/220px-KangnidoMap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/KangnidoMap.jpg/330px-KangnidoMap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/KangnidoMap.jpg/440px-KangnidoMap.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KangnidoMap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kangnido_map" title="Kangnido map">Kangnido map</a> (1402) predates Zheng's voyages and suggests that he had quite detailed geographical information on much of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> and moderate information on the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FraMauro1420Ship.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1286" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/FraMauro1420Ship.png/220px-FraMauro1420Ship.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/FraMauro1420Ship.png/330px-FraMauro1420Ship.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/FraMauro1420Ship.png/440px-FraMauro1420Ship.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FraMauro1420Ship.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of the <a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_map" title="Fra Mauro map">Fra Mauro map</a> relating the travels of a <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">junk</a> into the Atlantic Ocean in 1420. The ship also is illustrated above the text.</div></div></div>
<p>Zheng He generally sought to attain his goals through diplomacy, and his large army awed most would-be enemies into submission. However, a contemporary reported that Zheng He "walked like a tiger" and did not shrink from violence when he considered it necessary to impress foreign peoples with China's military might.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup> He ruthlessly suppressed pirates, who had long plagued Chinese and Southeast Asian waters. For example, he defeated <a href="/wiki/Chen_Zuyi" title="Chen Zuyi">Chen Zuyi</a>, one of the most feared and respected pirate captains, and returned him to China for execution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup> He also waged a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming-Kotte_War" title="Ming-Kotte War">land war</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kotte" title="Kingdom of Kotte">Kingdom of Kotte</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a>, and he made displays of military force when local officials threatened his fleet in Arabia and East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPereira2012265_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPereira2012265-72">[68]</a></sup> From his fourth voyage, he brought envoys from 30 states, who traveled to China and paid their respects at the Ming court.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 1424, the Yongle Emperor died. His successor, the <a href="/wiki/Hongxi_Emperor" title="Hongxi Emperor">Hongxi Emperor</a> (r. 1424–1425), stopped the voyages during his short reign. Zheng He made one more voyage during the reign of Hongxi's son, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor_of_China" title="Xuande Emperor of China">Xuande Emperor</a> (r. 1426–1435) but, the voyages of the Chinese treasure ship fleets then ended. Xuande believed his father's decision to halt the voyages had been meritorious and thus "there would be no need to make a detailed description of his grandfather's sending Zheng He to the Western Ocean."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Attribution_needed" title="Wikipedia:Attribution needed"><span title="This sentence may contain perspectives and opinions without stating whose they are. (March 2019)">attribution needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuiChunHing_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-HuiChunHing-54">[50]</a></sup> The voyages "were contrary to the rules stipulated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang_Ming_Zuxun" title="Huang Ming Zuxun">Huang Ming Zuxun</a>" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">皇明祖訓</span></span>), the dynastic foundation documents laid down by the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuiChunHing_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-HuiChunHing-54">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Some far-off countries pay their tribute to me at much expense and through great difficulties, all of which are by no means my own wish. Messages should be forwarded to them to reduce their tribute so as to avoid high and unnecessary expenses on both sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>They further violated longstanding Confucian principles. They were only made possible by (and therefore continued to represent) a triumph of the Ming's eunuch faction over the administration's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrat" title="Scholar-bureaucrat">scholar-bureaucrats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang1974_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang1974-52">[48]</a></sup> Upon Zheng He's death and his faction's fall from power, his successors sought to minimize him in official accounts, along with continuing attempts to destroy all records related to the Jianwen Emperor or the manhunt to find him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuiChunHing_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-HuiChunHing-54">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although unmentioned in the official dynastic histories, Zheng He probably died during the treasure fleet's last voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang1974_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang1974-52">[48]</a></sup> Although he has a tomb in China, it is empty since he was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buried_at_sea" title="Buried at sea">buried at sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E3%80%8A%E9%84%AD%E5%92%8C%E6%AD%B8%E4%BE%86%E3%80%8B%E3%80%82_%E8%97%9D%E8%A1%93%E5%AE%B6%E5%BC%97%E6%8B%89%E5%9F%BA%E7%B1%B3%E7%88%BE%C2%B7%E7%A7%91%E7%B4%A2%E5%A4%AB_2018.jpg"><img alt="Treasury ship.Zheng He." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2345" data-file-width="3989" decoding="async" height="129"
