srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/%E3%80%8A%E9%84%AD%E5%92%8C%E6%AD%B8%E4%BE%86%E3%80%8B%E3%80%82_%E8%97%9D%E8%A1%93%E5%AE%B6%E5%BC%97%E6%8B%89%E5%9F%BA%E7%B1%B3%E7%88%BE%C2%B7%E7%A7%91%E7%B4%A2%E5%A4%AB_2018.jpg/330px-%E3%80%8A%E9%84%AD%E5%92%8C%E6%AD%B8%E4%BE%86%E3%80%8B%E3%80%82_%E8%97%9D%E8%A1%93%E5%AE%B6%E5%BC%97%E6%8B%89%E5%9F%BA%E7%B1%B3%E7%88%BE%C2%B7%E7%A7%91%E7%B4%A2%E5%A4%AB_2018.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/%E3%80%8A%E9%84%AD%E5%92%8C%E6%AD%B8%E4%BE%86%E3%80%8B%E3%80%82_%E8%97%9D%E8%A1%93%E5%AE%B6%E5%BC%97%E6%8B%89%E5%9F%BA%E7%B1%B3%E7%88%BE%C2%B7%E7%A7%91%E7%B4%A2%E5%A4%AB_2018.jpg/440px-%E3%80%8A%E9%84%AD%E5%92%8C%E6%AD%B8%E4%BE%86%E3%80%8B%E3%80%82_%E8%97%9D%E8%A1%93%E5%AE%B6%E5%BC%97%E6%8B%89%E5%9F%BA%E7%B1%B3%E7%88%BE%C2%B7%E7%A7%91%E7%B4%A2%E5%A4%AB_2018.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E3%80%8A%E9%84%AD%E5%92%8C%E6%AD%B8%E4%BE%86%E3%80%8B%E3%80%82_%E8%97%9D%E8%A1%93%E5%AE%B6%E5%BC%97%E6%8B%89%E5%9F%BA%E7%B1%B3%E7%88%BE%C2%B7%E7%A7%91%E7%B4%A2%E5%A4%AB_2018.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's illustration of Zheng He's fleet</div></div></div>
<p>Zheng He led seven expeditions to the "Western" or Indian Ocean. Zheng He brought back to China many trophies and envoys from more than thirty kingdoms, including King <a href="/wiki/Vira_Alakesvara_of_Gampola" title="Vira Alakesvara of Gampola">Vira Alakeshwara</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a>, who came to China as a captive to apologize to the Emperor for offenses against his mission.
</p><p>Zheng He wrote of his travels:
</p>
<blockquote><p>We have traversed more than 100,000 <i><a href="/wiki/Li_(unit)" title="Li (unit)">li</a></i> of immense water spaces and have beheld in the ocean huge waves like mountains rising in the sky, and we have set eyes on barbarian regions far away hidden in a blue transparency of light vapors, while our sails, loftily unfurled like clouds day and night, continued their course [as rapidly] as a star, traversing those savage waves as if we were treading a public thoroughfare....<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sailing_charts">Sailing charts</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_geography" title="Chinese geography">Chinese geography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mao_Kun_map" title="Mao Kun map">Mao Kun map</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WuBeiZhi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="562" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/WuBeiZhi.jpg/220px-WuBeiZhi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/WuBeiZhi.jpg/330px-WuBeiZhi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/WuBeiZhi.jpg/440px-WuBeiZhi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WuBeiZhi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of a set of maps of Zheng He's missions (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">郑和航海图</span></span>), also known as the <a href="/wiki/Mao_Kun_map" title="Mao Kun map">Mao Kun map</a>, 1628</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1036" data-file-width="1454" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/220px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/330px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/440px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A section of the <i>Wubei Zhi</i> oriented east: India in the upper left, Sri Lanka upper right, and Africa along the bottom.</div></div></div>
<p>Zheng He's sailing charts, the <a href="/wiki/Mao_Kun_map" title="Mao Kun map">Mao Kun map</a>, were published in a book entitled the <i><a href="/wiki/Wubei_Zhi" title="Wubei Zhi">Wubei Zhi</a></i> (<i>A Treatise on Armament Technology</i>) written in 1621 and published in 1628 but traced back to Zheng He's and earlier voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup> It was originally a strip map 20.5 cm by 560 cm that could be rolled up, but was divided into 40 pages which vary in scale from 7 miles/inch in the Nanjing area to 215 miles/inch in parts of the African coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills1970_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills1970-69">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Investigation into folios 19V to 20R of the Mao Kun Map which covers the Indian Ocean including South India, Sri Lanka, the Maldives, and East Africa suggests that it is a composite of four maps, one for Sri Lanka, one for South India one for the Maldives and one for around 400 km of the East African coast, no further south than 6 degrees south of the Equator. Each of these maps is positioned at a different orientation to fit with the ocean currents and winds required of a sailing chart, rather than a formal map. The analysis also suggests that Arabic-speaking pilots with a detailed knowledge of the African coast were involved in the cartography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPereira2012273–277_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPereira2012273–277-77">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is little attempt to provide an accurate 2-D representation; instead, the sailing instructions are given using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_directions" title="Chinese directions">24-point compass system</a> with a Chinese symbol for each point, together with a sailing time or distance, which takes account of the local currents and winds. Sometimes depth soundings are also provided. It also shows bays, estuaries, capes and islands, ports and mountains along the coast, important landmarks such as pagodas and temples, and shoal rocks. Of 300 named places outside China, more than 80% can be confidently located. There are also fifty observations of stellar altitude.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_of_ships">Size of ships</span></h2>
<p>According to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Luo_Maodeng&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Luo Maodeng (page does not exist)">Luo Maodeng</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E7%BE%85%E6%87%8B%E7%99%BB" title="zh:羅懋登">zh</a>]</span>'s novel <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sanbao_Taijian_Xia_Xiyang_Ji_Tongsu_Yanyi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sanbao Taijian Xia Xiyang Ji Tongsu Yanyi (page does not exist)">Sanbao Taijian Xia Xiyang Ji Tongsu Yanyi</a></i> (1597), the first expedition had:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007104_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007104-78">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>"<a href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_ship" title="Chinese treasure ship">Chinese treasure ships</a>" (<span lang="zh-Hant">宝船</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Bǎo Chuán</span></i>), used by the commander of the fleet and his deputies, nine-masted, about 127 metres (417 feet) long, 52 metres (171 feet) wide, with four decks.</li>
<li>Equine ships (<span lang="zh-Hant">馬船</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Mǎ Chuán</span></i>), carrying horses and tribute goods and repair material for the fleet, eight-masted, about 103 m (338 ft) long and 42 m (138 ft) wide.</li>
<li>Supply ships (<span lang="zh-Hant">粮船</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Liáng Chuán</span></i>), containing staple for the crew, seven-masted, about 78 m (256 ft) long and 35 m (115 ft) wide.</li>
<li>Troop transports (<span lang="zh-Hant">兵船</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Bīng Chuán</span></i>), six-masted, about 67 m (220 ft) long and 25 m (82 ft) wide.</li>
<li>Fuchuan warships (<span lang="zh-Hant">福船</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Fú Chuán</span></i>), five-masted, about 50 m (160 ft) long.</li>
<li>Patrol boats (<span lang="zh-Hant">坐船</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Zuò Chuán</span></i>), eight-oared, about 37 m (121 ft) long.</li>
<li>Water tankers (<span lang="zh-Hant">水船</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Shuǐ Chuán</span></i>), with 1 month's supply of fresh water.</li></ul>
<p>On the ships were navigators, explorers, sailors, doctors, workers, and soldiers, along with the translator and diarist <a href="/wiki/Gong_Zhen" title="Gong Zhen">Gong Zhen</a>. Six more expeditions took place from 1407 to 1433, with fleets thought to be of comparable size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007-79">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a> both described multi-masted ships carrying 500 to 1,000 passengers in their translated accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1971460–470_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1971460–470-80">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_de%27_Conti" title="Niccolò de' Conti">Niccolò de' Conti</a>, a contemporary of Zheng He, was also an eyewitness of ships in Southeast Asia, claiming to have seen five-masted junks weighing about 2,000 <i>vegetes</i>, that is <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venetian</a> butt. Christopher Wake estimated a burthen of 1300 tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWake199758_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWake199758-81">[77]</a></sup> The ship of Conti may have been a Burmese or Indonesian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jong_(ship)" title="Jong (ship)">jong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis1973248_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis1973248-82">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest ships in the fleet, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_ships" title="Chinese treasure ships">Chinese treasure ships</a> described in Chinese chronicles, would have been nearly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_world%27s_largest_wooden_ships" title="List of world's largest wooden ships">twice as long as any other wooden ship</a> recorded thereafter until the 20th century, surpassing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Admiral_Nelson" title="Admiral Nelson">Admiral Nelson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/HMS_Victory" title="HMS Victory">HMS <i>Victory</i></a>, 69.34 metres (227 ft 6 in) long, which was launched in 1765, and the 68.88-metre (226 ft 0 in) <a href="/wiki/Vasa_(ship)" title="Vasa (ship)"><i>Vasa</i></a> of 1627. The first ships to attain 126 m (413 ft) long were 19th century steamers with iron hulls. Many scholars consider it unlikely that any of Zheng He's ships were 135 m (450 ft) in length and have proposed much shorter lengths, as low as 60–75 m (200–250 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChurch2005_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChurch2005-83">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disputes_of_historical_records_of_length">Disputes of historical records of length</span></h3>
<p>Edward L. Dreyer claims that Luo Maodeng's novel is unsuitable as historical evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007104_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007104-78">[74]</a></sup> The novel contains a number of fantasy element; for example the ships were "constructed with divine help by the immortal Lu Ban".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChurch20057_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChurch20057-84">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>One explanation for the seemingly-inefficient size of the colossal ships was that the 44 largest Zhang treasure ships were used only by the Emperor and imperial bureaucrats to travel along the Yangtze for court business, including reviewing Zheng He's expedition fleet. The Yangtze river, with its calmer waters, may have been navigable by these treasure ships. Zheng He, a court eunuch, would not have had the privilege in rank to command the largest of the ships, seaworthy or not. The main ships of Zheng He's fleet were instead six-masted 2000-liao ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical1_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Technical1-85">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup> That would give <a href="/wiki/Builder%27s_Old_Measurement" title="Builder's Old Measurement">burthen</a> of 500 tons and a displacement tonnage of about 800 tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical1_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Technical1-85">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1971481_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1971481-87">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional and popular accounts of Zheng He's voyages have described a great fleet of gigantic ships far larger than any other wooden ships in history. The most grandiose claims for Zheng He's 1405 fleet are entirely based on a calculation derived from an account that was written three centuries later and was accepted as fact by one modern writer; rejected by numerous naval experts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 128">: 128 </span></sup> There are even some sources that claim some of the treasure ships might have been as long as 183 m (600 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[85]</a></sup> The claims that the Chinese treasure ships reached such size is disputed because other 17th century Ming records stated that European <a href="/wiki/East_Indiaman" title="East Indiaman">East Indiamen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galleon" title="Galleon">galleons</a> are 30, 40, 50, and 60 <i>zhang</i> (90, 120, 150, and 180 m) in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENaiming201654–55_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENaiming201654–55-90">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treasure_Shipyard_excavation">Treasure Shipyard excavation</span></h3>
<p>From 2003 to 2004, the Treasure Shipyard was excavated in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> (the former capital of the Ming Dynasty), near the Yangtze River. Despite the site being referred to as the "Longjiang Treasure Shipyard" (龍江寶船廠) in the official names, the site is distinct from the actual Longjiang Shipyard, which was located on a different site and produced different types of ships. The Treasure Shipyard, where Zheng He's fleet were believed to have been built in the Ming Dynasty, once consisted of thirteen basins (based on a 1944 map), most of which have now been covered by the construction of buildings in the 20th century. The basins are believed to have been connected to the Yangtze via a series of gates. Three long basins survive, each with wooden structures inside them that were interpreted to be frames for the ships to be built on. The largest basin extends for a length of 421 metres (1,381 ft). While they were long enough to accommodate the largest claimed Zheng He treasure ship, they were not wide enough to fit even a ship half the claimed size. The basin was only 41 metres (135 ft) wide at most, with only a 10 metres (33 ft) width area of it showing evidence of structures. They were also not deep enough, being only 4 metres (13 ft) deep. Other remains of ships in the site indicate that the ships were only slightly larger than the frames that supported them. Moreover, the basin structures were grouped into clusters with large gaps between them, if each cluster was interpreted as a ship framework, then the largest ship would not exceed 75 metres (246 ft) at most, probably less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-church2010_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-church2010-91">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2003–2004 excavation also recovered two complete wooden rudderposts from the Treasure Shipyard, in addition to another recovered in 1957. They are made of <a href="/wiki/Teak" title="Teak">teak</a> and measured around 10 to 11 metres (33 to 36 ft) in length. Zhou Shide (1962) claimed that the first rudderpost recovered was proof of the enormous dimensions of the ships based on his calculations on how big the rudderblade would be. However Church (2010) points out that Zhou was using calculations based on modern steel propeller-driven ships, not wooden ships; as well as the fact that Zhou's hypothetical rudder shape was based on the flat-bottomed <i>shachuan</i> (沙船) ship type, not the sea-going <i>fuchuan</i> (福船). The rudderposts can not be used to infer the actual size of the rudder blades. Church notes that in traditional wooden Chinese ships, rudderposts were necessarily long in order for them to extend from the water level up unto the ship deck, where it was controlled by the tiller. Church compares it with modern wooden junks built in the traditional <i>Lümeimao</i> ("green eyebrow", 綠眉毛) style, which also have rudderposts that are 11 metres (36 ft) long, but are only 31 metres (102 ft) in overall length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-church2010_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-church2010-91">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<p>One theory is that Admiral Zheng He died in 1433, during or shortly after the seventh voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007165_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer2007165-92">[88]</a></sup> Another is that Zheng He continued to serve as the defender of Nanjing, dying in 1435.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills19706_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills19706-93">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>A tomb was built for Zheng He at the southern slope of Cattle Head Hill, <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>. The original tomb was a horseshoe-shaped grave. It is a <a href="/wiki/Cenotaph" title="Cenotaph">cenotaph</a> believed to contain his clothes and headgear. In 1985, the tomb was rebuilt following a Muslim style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lin05_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-lin05-94">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<p>Zheng's voyages were long neglected in official Chinese histories but have become well known in China and abroad since the publication of <a href="/wiki/Liang_Qichao" title="Liang Qichao">Liang Qichao</a>'s <i>Biography of Our Homeland's Great Navigator, Zheng He</i> in 1904.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMZX_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-HMZX-96">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_China">Imperial China</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chen_Zhang%27s_painting_of_a_giraffe_and_its_attendant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="446" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Chen_Zhang%27s_painting_of_a_giraffe_and_its_attendant.jpg/170px-Chen_Zhang%27s_painting_of_a_giraffe_and_its_attendant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8f/Chen_Zhang%27s_painting_of_a_giraffe_and_its_attendant.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chen_Zhang%27s_painting_of_a_giraffe_and_its_attendant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The pet giraffe of the <a href="/wiki/Ghiyasuddin_Azam_Shah" title="Ghiyasuddin Azam Shah">Sultan of Bengal</a>, brought from the Somali <a href="/wiki/Ajuran_Sultanate" title="Ajuran Sultanate">Ajuran Empire</a>, and later taken to China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup> in the thirteenth year of Yongle (1415).</div></div></div>
<p>In the decades after the last voyage, Imperial officials minimized the importance of Zheng He and his expeditions throughout the many regnal and dynastic histories they compiled. The information in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Taizong_Shilu" title="Ming Taizong Shilu">Yongle</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Xuanzong_Shilu" title="Ming Xuanzong Shilu">Xuande Emperors' official annals</a> was incomplete and even erroneous, and other official publications omitted them completely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMZX_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-HMZX-96">[92]</a></sup> Although some have seen that as a conspiracy seeking to eliminate memories of the voyages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heyhei_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-heyhei-98">[94]</a></sup> it is likely that the records were dispersed throughout several departments and the expeditions, unauthorized by and in fact counter to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang_Ming_Zu_Xun" title="Huang Ming Zu Xun">injunctions of the dynastic founder</a>, presented a kind of embarrassment to the dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMZX_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-HMZX-96">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>State-sponsored Ming naval efforts declined dramatically after Zheng's voyages. Starting in the early 15th century, China experienced increasing pressure from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Yuan_dynasty" title="Northern Yuan dynasty">surviving Yuan Mongols</a> from the north. The relocation of the capital to Beijing in the north exacerbated this threat dramatically. At considerable expense, China launched annual military expeditions from Beijing to weaken the Mongolians. The expenditures necessary for the land campaigns directly competed with the funds necessary to continue naval expeditions. Further, in 1449, Mongolian cavalry ambushed a land expedition personally led by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhengtong_Emperor" title="Zhengtong Emperor">Zhengtong Emperor</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tumu_Fortress" title="Battle of Tumu Fortress">Tumu Fortress</a>, less than a day's march from the walls of the capital. The Mongolians wiped out the Chinese army and captured the emperor. The battle had two salient effects. Firstly, it demonstrated the clear threat posed by the northern nomads. Secondly, the Mongols caused a political crisis in China when they released the emperor after his half-brother had already ascended and declared the new <a href="/wiki/Jingtai_Emperor" title="Jingtai Emperor">Jingtai era</a>. Not until 1457 and the restoration of the former emperor would political stability return. Upon his return to power, China abandoned the strategy of annual land expeditions and instead embarked upon a massive and expensive expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a>. In that environment, funding for naval expeditions was simply absent.
</p><p>However, missions from Southeastern Asia continued to arrive for decades. Depending on local conditions, they could reach such frequency that the court found it necessary to restrict them. The <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Ming" title="History of Ming">History of Ming</a></i> records imperial edicts forbade Java, Champa, and Siam from sending their envoys more often than once every three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-99">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2362" data-file-width="3543" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg/220px-Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg/330px-Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg/440px-Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ông Bổn Temple, built to venerate Zheng He, or Bổn Đầu Công in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saigon" title="Saigon">Saigon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Veneration">Veneration</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cakra_Donya.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1248" data-file-width="1664" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Cakra_Donya.JPG/220px-Cakra_Donya.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Cakra_Donya.JPG/330px-Cakra_Donya.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Cakra_Donya.JPG/440px-Cakra_Donya.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cakra_Donya.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cakra Donya Bell, a gift from Zheng He to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pasai" title="Pasai">Pasai</a>, now kept at the <a href="/wiki/Aceh_Museum" title="Aceh Museum">Aceh Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Banda_Aceh" title="Banda Aceh">Banda Aceh</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_diaspora" title="Chinese diaspora">Chinese diaspora</a> in Southeast Asia, Zheng He became a figure of folk veneration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup> Even some of his crew members who happened to stay in some port sometimes did so as well, such as "Poontaokong" on <a href="/wiki/Sulu_Archipelago" title="Sulu Archipelago">Sulu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-99">[95]</a></sup> The temples of the cult, called after either of his names, Cheng Hoon or Sam Po, are peculiar to overseas Chinese except for a single temple in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hongjian&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hongjian (page does not exist)">Hongjian</a> originally constructed by a returned <a href="/wiki/Filipinos" title="Filipinos">Filipino</a> Chinese in the Ming dynasty and rebuilt by another Filipino Chinese after the original was destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-99">[95]</a></sup> (The same village of Hongjian, in <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Jiaomei" title="Jiaomei">Jiaomei</a> township, is also the ancestral home of former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philippine" title="Philippine">Philippine</a> President <a href="/wiki/Corazon_Aquino" title="Corazon Aquino">Corazon Aquino</a>.)
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malacca">Malacca</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG/220px-%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG/330px-%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG/440px-%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Poh_San_Teng_Temple" title="Poh San Teng Temple">Sam Po Kong Temple</a> in Malacca.</div></div></div>
<p>The oldest and most important Chinese temple in Malacca is the 17th-century Cheng Hoon Teng, dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Guanyin" title="Guanyin">Guanyin</a>. During <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch colonial rule</a>, the head of the Cheng Hoon Temple was appointed as chief over the community's Chinese inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-99">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following Zheng He's arrival, the sultan and the sultana of Malacca visited China at the head of over 540 of their subjects, bearing ample tribute. Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mansur_Shah_of_Malacca" title="Mansur Shah of Malacca">Mansur Shah</a> (r. 1459–1477) later dispatched Tun Perpatih Putih as his envoy to China, carrying a letter from the sultan to the Ming emperor. The letter requested the hand of an imperial daughter in marriage. Malay (but not Chinese) annals record that in 1459, a princess named <a href="/wiki/Hang_Li_Po" title="Hang Li Po">Hang Li Po</a> or Hang Liu was sent from China to marry the sultan. She came with 500 high-ranking young men and a few hundred handmaidens as her entourage. They eventually settled in <a href="/wiki/Bukit_Cina" title="Bukit Cina">Bukit Cina</a>. It is believed that a significant number of them married into the local populace, creating the descendants now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peranakan" title="Peranakan">Peranakan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malacca_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-malacca-101">[97]</a></sup> Owing to this supposed lineage, the Peranakan still use special honorifics: <i><a href="/wiki/Baba_(honorific)" title="Baba (honorific)">Baba</a></i> for the men and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nyonya" title="Nyonya">Nyonya</a></i> for the women.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">Indonesia</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stamps_of_Indonesia,_026-05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="306" data-file-width="499" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg/220px-Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg/330px-Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg/440px-Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stamps_of_Indonesia,_026-05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stamp from <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> commemorating Zheng He's voyages to secure the maritime routes, usher urbanization and assist in creating a common prosperity throughout continents and cultures.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cheng_Hoo1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="923" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Cheng_Hoo1.JPG/220px-Cheng_Hoo1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Cheng_Hoo1.JPG/330px-Cheng_Hoo1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Cheng_Hoo1.JPG/440px-Cheng_Hoo1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cheng_Hoo1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Zheng Hoo Mosque in <a href="/wiki/Surabaya" title="Surabaya">Surabaya</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indonesian_Chinese" title="Indonesian Chinese">Chinese Indonesian</a> community have established temples dedicated to Zheng He in <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cirebon" title="Cirebon">Cirebon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surabaya" title="Surabaya">Surabaya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Semarang" title="Semarang">Semarang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_99-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-99">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1961, the Indonesian Islamic leader and scholar <a href="/wiki/Hamka" title="Hamka">Hamka</a> credited Zheng He for playing an important role in the development of Islam in Indonesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup> The <i>Brunei Times</i> credits Zheng He with building Chinese Muslim communities in <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">Palembang</a> and along the shores of <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>. These Muslims allegedly followed the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> school in the Chinese language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup> The <i>Malay Annals</i> also record a number of Hanafi mosques – in <a href="/wiki/Semarang" title="Semarang">Semarang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancol" title="Ancol">Ancol</a>, for instance – were converted directly into temples of the Zheng He cult during the 1460s and the 1470s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_99-5"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-99">[95]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sam_Poo_Kong" title="Sam Poo Kong">Sam Poo Kong</a> temple in Semarang was built to commemorate Zheng He's voyage to Java.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_scholarship">Modern scholarship</span></h3>
<p>In the 1950s, historians such as <a href="/wiki/John_King_Fairbank" title="John King Fairbank">John Fairbank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Needham" title="Joseph Needham">Joseph Needham</a> popularized the idea that after Zheng He's voyages China turned away from the seas due to the <i><a href="/wiki/Haijin" title="Haijin">Haijin</a></i> edict and was isolated from European technological advancements. Modern historians point out that Chinese maritime commerce did not totally stop after Zheng He, that Chinese ships continued to participate in Southeast Asian commerce until the 19th century, and that active Chinese trading with India and East Africa continued long after the time of Zheng. Moreover, <a href="/wiki/Historical_revisionism" title="Historical revisionism">revisionist historians</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Jack_Goldstone" title="Jack Goldstone">Jack Goldstone</a> argue that the Zheng He voyages ended for practical reasons that did not reflect the technological level of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup> Although the Ming dynasty prohibited shipping with the <i>Haijin</i> edict, it was a policy of the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a> that long preceded Zheng He and the ban, so obviously disregarded by the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a>, was eventually lifted entirely. However, the ban on maritime shipping forced countless numbers of people into smuggling and <a href="/wiki/Wokou" title="Wokou">piracy</a>. Neglect of the imperial navy and Nanjing dockyards after Zheng He's voyages left the coast highly vulnerable to Japanese <a href="/wiki/Wokou" title="Wokou">wokou</a> during the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang2013_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang2013-105">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grygiel2006_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grygiel2006-106">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Richard von Glahn, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UCLA" title="UCLA">UCLA</a> professor of Chinese history, commented that most treatments of Zheng He present him wrongly, "offer counterfactual arguments," and "emphasize China's missed opportunity" by focusing on failures, instead of accomplishments. In contrast, Glahn asserts that "Zheng He reshaped Asia" because maritime history in the 15th century was essentially the Zheng He story and the effects of his voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glaan_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-glaan-107">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_influence">Cultural influence</span></h3>
<p>Despite the official neglect, the adventures of the fleet captured the imagination of some Chinese with some writing novelizations of the voyages, such as the <i>Romance of the Three-Jeweled Eunuch</i> in 1597.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heyhei_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-heyhei-98">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>On his travels, Zheng He built mosques<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[104]</a></sup> and also spread the worship of <a href="/wiki/Mazu" title="Mazu">Mazu</a>. He apparently never found time for a <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">pilgrimage</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> but sent sailors there on his last voyage. He played an important part in developing relations between China and Islamic countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup> Zheng He also visited Muslim shrines of Islamic holy men in the <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In modern times, interest in Zheng He has revived substantially. In <a href="/wiki/Vernor_Vinge" title="Vernor Vinge">Vernor Vinge</a>'s 1999 science fiction novel <i><a href="/wiki/A_Deepness_in_the_Sky" title="A Deepness in the Sky">A Deepness in the Sky</a></i>, an interstellar society of commercial traders in human space are named the Qeng Ho, after the admiral. The expeditions featured prominently in <a href="/wiki/Heather_Terrell" title="Heather Terrell">Heather Terrell</a>'s 2005 novel <i>The Map Thief</i>. For the 600th anniversary of Zheng He's voyages in 2005, <a href="/wiki/China_Central_Television" title="China Central Television">China Central Television</a> produced a special television series, <i><a href="/wiki/Zheng_He_Xia_Xiyang_(TV_series)" title="Zheng He Xia Xiyang (TV series)">Zheng He Xia Xiyang</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Gallen_Lo" title="Gallen Lo">Gallen Lo</a> as Zheng He. He is also mentioned in part of the main storyline of the first-person shooter game <a href="/wiki/Far_Cry_3" title="Far Cry 3">Far Cry 3</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek" title="Star Trek">Star Trek</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_Picard" title="Star Trek: Picard">Picard</a></i> further featured an advanced starship named USS <i>Zheng He</i>. There was even a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Navy" title="US Navy">US Navy</a> boat that was acquired for picket duty during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> that was named <a href="/wiki/USS_Cheng_Ho_(IX-52)" title="USS Cheng Ho (IX-52)"><i>Cheng Ho</i></a> by its previous owner.  In <a href="/wiki/Civilization_VI" title="Civilization VI">Civilization VI</a>, Zheng He is a 'great admiral' unit that grants bonuses to trade and naval combat.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relics">Relics</span></h3>
<dl><dt>Nanjing Temple of Mazu</dt></dl>
<p>Zheng He built the Tianfei Palace (<span lang="zh-Hant">天妃宫</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Tiānfēigōng</span></i>; 'Palace of the Celestial Wife'), a temple in honor of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mythology" title="Chinese mythology">goddess</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Mazu</a>, in Nanjing after the fleet returned from its first western voyage in 1407.
</p>
<dl><dt>Taicang Stele</dt></dl>
<p>The "Deed of Foreign Connection and Exchange" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">通番事跡</span></span>) or "Tongfan Deed Stele" is located in the Tianfei Palace in <a href="/wiki/Liuhe,_Taicang" title="Liuhe, Taicang">Liuhe, Taicang</a>, whence the expeditions first departed. The <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> was submerged and lost but has been rebuilt.
</p>
<dl><dt>Nanshan Stele</dt></dl>
<p>To thank the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Celestial Wife</a> for her blessings, Zheng He and his colleagues rebuilt the Tianfei Palace in Nanshan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Changle" title="Changle">Changle</a> County, Fujian Province as well before they left on their last voyage. At the renovated temple, they raised a stele, "A Record of Tianfei Showing Her Presence and Power" (<span lang="zh-Hant">天妃靈應之記</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Tiānfēi Líng Yīng zhī Jì</span></i>), discussing their earlier voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Sri Lankan Stele</dt></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Galle_Trilingual_Inscription" title="Galle Trilingual Inscription">Galle Trilingual Inscription</a> in Sri Lanka was discovered in the city of <a href="/wiki/Galle" title="Galle">Galle</a> in 1911 and is preserved at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Colombo" title="National Museum of Colombo">National Museum of Colombo</a>. The three languages used in the inscription were Chinese, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>. The inscription praises <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> and describes the fleet's donations to the famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tondeswaram_temple" title="Tondeswaram temple">Tenavarai Nayanar temple</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tondeswaram" title="Tondeswaram">Tondeswaram</a> frequented by both <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Tomb and Museum</dt></dl>
<p>Zheng He's tomb in Nanjing has been repaired and a small museum built next to it, but his body was buried at sea off the <a href="/wiki/Malabar_Coast" title="Malabar Coast">Malabar Coast</a> near Calicut, in western India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes1996172_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes1996172-115">[111]</a></sup> However, his sword and other personal possessions were interred in a Muslim tomb inscribed in <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>.
</p><p>The tomb of Zheng He's assistant <a href="/wiki/Hong_Bao" title="Hong Bao">Hong Bao</a> was recently unearthed in Nanjing as well.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span></h3>
<p>In the People's Republic of China, 11 July is <a href="/wiki/China_National_Maritime_Day" title="China National Maritime Day">Maritime Day</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">中国航海日</span></span>, <i>Zhōngguó Hánghǎi Rì</i>) and is devoted to the memory of Zheng He's first voyage. Initially <a href="/wiki/Kunming_Changshui_International_Airport" title="Kunming Changshui International Airport">Kunming Changshui International Airport</a> was to be named Zheng He International Airport.
</p><p>In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Emotion_Media_Factory" title="Emotion Media Factory">Emotion Media Factory</a> dedicated a special multimedia show "Zheng He is coming" for amusement park <a href="/wiki/Romon_U-Park" title="Romon U-Park">Romon U-Park</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ningbo" title="Ningbo">Ningbo</a>, China). The show became a finalist of the amusement industry prestigious Brass Rings Awards by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAAPA" title="IAAPA">IAAPA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zheng He is the namesake of the <a href="/wiki/ROCS_Cheng_Ho_(PFG2-1103)" title="ROCS Cheng Ho (PFG2-1103)">ROCS <i>Cheng Ho</i></a> missile frigate in Taiwan.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army_Navy" title="People's Liberation Army Navy">People's Liberation Army Navy</a> ship <i><a href="/wiki/Type_679_training_ship" title="Type 679 training ship">Zheng He (AX-81)</a></i> is a Chinese <a href="/wiki/Training_ship" title="Training ship">training ship</a> named for him. Like her namesake, she serves as a goodwill ambassador for China, becoming the first Chinese Navy ship to visit the United States in 1989 and completing a circumnavigation of the globe in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>The proposed <a href="/wiki/Sample-return_mission" title="Sample-return mission">sample-return</a> spacecraft <i><a href="/wiki/Tianwen-2" title="Tianwen-2">Tianwen-2</a></i> was originally named <i>ZhenghHe</i>.
Its mission to explore <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object#Near-Earth_asteroids" title="Near-Earth object">Near-Earth asteroid</a> <a href="/wiki/469219_Kamo%CA%BBoalewa" title="469219 Kamoʻoalewa">2016 HO3</a> is scheduled to launch in 2024.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>Taizong Shilu</i> 27 February 1424 entry reports that Zheng He was sent to deliver the seal because the old seal was destroyed in a fire. The <i>Xuanzong Shilu</i> 17 September 1425 entry reports that Zhang Funama delivered a seal, because the old seal was destroyed in a fire. The later <i>Mingshi</i> compilers seem to have combined the accounts, remarking that Shi Jisun's succession was approved in 1424 and that a new seal was delivered in 1425, suggesting that only one seal was destroyed by fire. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyer200796_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyer200796-38">[36]</a></sup></span>
